[HEADING] Project Abstract Summary

[TEXT] Funding Opportunity Number HRSA-26-004

[TEXT] CFDA(s) 93.224

[TEXT] Applicant Name

[TEXT] FAMILY PRACTICE & COUNSELING SERVICES NETWORK INC

[TEXT] Descriptive Title of Applicant's Project

[TEXT] Health Center Cluster

[HEADING] Project Abstract

[TEXT] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network (FPCSN or the Network) is submitting this Service Area Competition New (SAAT ID #154 ***; Philadelphia, Pennsylvania) application to continue services to residents of public housing and surrounding communities. For the last 34 years, the Network was an FQHC under Resources for Human Development (previous Grant No. H80CS00718). That organization changed ownership and relinquished the grant on 6/30/25. The health center has established the new FPCSN non-profit corporation, which meets all of the requirements of the HRSA Health Center Program, and is now applying as a new applicant for the area it has served for many years. This application is being submitted to ensure that patients continue to have access to the vital services FPCSN provides.FPCSN serves a large part of urban Philadelphia, where the residents are largely Black/African American (54.2%) and low income (49.6% under 200% of poverty). The community needs assessment shows significant disparities in access to health care and the health of residents, compared to the state. The target population has high levels of chronic illness, significant barriers to adequate health care, and a lack of culturally appropriate and accessible services. Much of the service area is designated as Medically Underserved Areas and as Dental Health Professional Shortage Areas. FPCSN collaborates with the Philadelphia Housing Authority and Department of Public Health and a number of local and state organizations, including the Pennsylvania Association of Community Health Centers. The Network includes three full comprehensive service sites: Abbottsford/Falls, the Health Annex, and the Stephen and Sandra Sheller 11th Street Family Health Services and six school-based sites. The Network's sites have all been developed in collaboration with public housing residents who are the target of services. FPCSN served 15,617 people in 2024, an increase from 15,214 in 2023 and 14,872 in 2022. One FPCSN site has Patient-Centered Medical Home recognition.FPCSN health center sites will continue to provide the full range of primary and preventive FQHSC services, including pre-natal, obstetric, and midwifery services; diabetes and nutrition education; podiatry, pharmacy; and case management. FPCSN provides behavioral health services and has been a leader in the integration of behavioral health with primary care. Medication for Opioid Use Disorder services began in 2018. A full range of oral health services is provided and accessible to all patients. In recent years, in recognition of the trauma and stress experienced by patients and staff, the health centers have added complementary wellness services, including mind/body care such as yoga, acupuncture, and massage. A longstanding continuous quality improvement system and a fully certified EHR assure quality services and efficiency. The FPSCN Board meets 330 requirements and is vested with full authority and responsibility for the operations of the FPCSN Health Center. The Board includes one member who is a representative of the Housing Authority and two others who are or have been public housing residents. FPCSN is working to increase the number of patients served, but is now planning to serve at least 15,617 patients in the 2026 calendar year.

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[HEADING] Introduction

[TEXT] Family Practice Counseling and Services Network, Inc. (FPCSN or the Network) is submitting this Service Area Competition New application to continue health care services to residents of public housing and surrounding underserved Philadelphia, Pennsylvania communities. For the last 33 years, the Network was an FQHC under Resources for Human Development. That organization changed ownership and relinquished the grant on 6/30/25. The FPCSN health center has established the new non-profit FPCSN corporation, registered in Pennsylvania and with the IRS, that meets all of the requirements of the HRSA Health Center Program. The area served includes large parts of urban Philadelphia that have concentrations of public housing. The FPCSN health center includes three comprehensive service sites: Abbottsford/Falls, the Health Annex, and the Stephen and Sandra Sheller 11th Street Family Health Services of Drexel University, as well as six school-based sites. FPCSN is the largest nurse-led health center network in the country, and one of the oldest. Over its long history, FPCSN has used an integrated model to deliver primary care provided by nurse practitioner, behavioral health, dental, and preventive services providers. The FPCSN model is a psychosocial, interdisciplinary approach that "wraps its arms" around patients. The FPCSN Board meets all 330 requirements.

[HEADING] NEED

[HEADING] 1. Service Area and Assessment of Community Served

[HEADING] a. Service Area Boundaries

[TEXT] The FPCSN service area includes 25 Zip Codes in Philadelphia City/County and four adjacent Delaware County Zip Codes where residents are largely low-income minorities. Almost all of the service area in Philadelphia is designated as Medically Underserved Area (#02920, 21, 22, 23; #02947 and 51; #03022, 23, and 25; and #04060)1. Most of the service area in Philadelphia is also designated as Dental Health Professional Shortage Area (Low income (#6426206525, #6421164833, #6428506949, and #6427043664)2,3. The GeoCare Navigator map document included in this application (Attachment 1) shows the Zip Codes (gray area) in the service area, the MUAs to be served (orange areas), and the area FQHC sites and hospitals4. The required map, legend, and table are included, as well as one map that shows the Dental HPSAs (purple area) and public housing and a second map that includes labels for the FPCSN sites and other FQHC sites and the hospitals serving the area.

[HEADING] b. Location in an Opportunity Zone

[TEXT] A number of the Census Tracts in the FPCSN service area in the Philadelphia and Delaware County area are designated as Opportunity Zones. These are noted in a last map in Attachment 15.

[HEADING] c. Service Area Ensures Service Accessibility

[TEXT] As noted on the map, the FPCSN health centers are located in the Northwest, North, and West/Southwest areas of Philadelphia, where most of the Philadelphia Housing Authority developments are located. The Delaware County area served is also where much of the public

[TEXT] 1 US Department of Health and Human Services, Health Resources and Services Administration, Data Warehouse, https://data.hrsa.gov/tools/shortage-area/mua-find, obtained August 2025

[TEXT] 2 US Department of Health and Human Services, Health Resources and Services Administration, Data Warehouse, https://data.hrsa.gov/tools/shortage-area/hpsa-find, obtained August 2025

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] housing is found in that county. FPCSN health center locations are accessible to public housing developments, either by walking or by public transportation. Both Philadelphia and the nearest parts of Delaware County have extensive public transportation, including SouthEast Pennsylvania Transit Authority (SEPTA) subways, buses, trolleys, and regional rail, so most patients from the areas served are able to travel to FPCSN services, sometimes with assistance from the health center. Patients with mobility difficulties or who must bring children to appointments are assisted to access transportation operated by their insurer or provided transportation directly using the FPCSN vans.

[HEADING] d. Overlap and Access to Primary Health Care Services

[TEXT] FPCSN has grown and developed over many years in response to need and demand in the communities served, particularly the public housing communities. The Network collaborates with the Philadelphia Housing Authority (PHA) to ensure that public housing residents are aware of the health center's services and to identify residents who are interested in working with the health center. In 2023, HUD awarded Philadelphia a Choice Neighborhoods Initiative (CNI) implementation grant to expand the Bartram Village development in Southwest Philadelphia. FPCSN was a partner in that project, providing health care, prenatal care, cancer screening, connection with services, training, and programming in the development. The FPCSN service area boundaries overlap those of seven of Philadelphia's 13 Health Planning Districts and the health center has always worked closely with the City Department of Public Health. Both organizations have also worked with the Promise Zone that has existed within the service area in West Philadelphia since 2014. In recent years, FPCSN has worked with the Philadelphia School District, which includes the entire City. The health center is registered as a provider with the School District and currently has six school-based health center sites in area schools. Delaware County is a suburban county that, as a whole, is very wealthy with a number of school districts across the county. However, the areas bordering Philadelphia have high levels of poverty and low penetration by FQHCs. Because there is only one FQHC that has locations in Delaware County that serve the general public and a number of hospitals have closed in recent years, many people from those bordering areas travel into Philadelphia for primary care.

[HEADING] e. Service Area Review

[TEXT] It is FPCSN policy that, as part of the process of needs assessment and strategic planning and review, management and the Board complete an annual review of the UDS report for previous years, including the Zip Codes in which patients reside. Recent reviews found that 84.6% of the 15,214 patients served in 2023 and 84.0% of the 15,617 patients served in 2024 resided in the Zip Codes noted on Form 5B, percentages that are higher than the 75% required. The annual review of patient origin information is used to identify any new areas where people are coming from for care. The health center then reviews need and resources in those areas and may add Zip Codes to the scope and work to increase relationships and outreach where services are needed.

[HEADING] f. Urban/Rural Selection

[TEXT] The urban/rural selection on the SAAT and Form 1A of this application are both urban.

[TEXT] g. Area Changes Since Last SAC Application

[TEXT] The service area 154 *** noted on the SAC SAAT is the area that has been served by the health center now known as FPCSN for many years. The previous awardee was Resources for Human Development and the health center was a program of that organization until its ownership

[TEXT] changed and it relinquished the grant on 6/30/2025. The health center has established the new non-profit FPCSN corporation and is required to apply for this service area as a New Applicant. However, because of its long history in serving this area as an FQHC, FPCSN is going to answer the questions for Competing Continuation Applicants as well as those for New Applicants.

[TEXT] The last SAC application for this service area submitted by the health center under the previous awardee was completed in October, 2024 for the 2025-28 period. There have been no significant changes to the service area, as can be noted by the fact that 84.6% of the 15,214 patients served in 2023 and 84.0% of the 15,617 patients served in 2024 resided in the service area Zip Codes listed on the SAAT and noted in Form 5B for the previous awardee and for this application. A change that is taking place among FPCSN patients and the service area alike is that an increasing number of patients do not have insurance coverage for their care and decreasing numbers are covered by Medicaid.

[HEADING] 2. Health Care Needs of the Service Area/Population

[TEXT] The 2023 US Census6 indicates that there are 1,016,690 people living in the FPCSN service area. The population has a level of poverty that is almost two and a half times that of the state and more than twice the percentages of children and elderly living in poverty than the state. In spite of ongoing efforts to reduce the number of people who lack health insurance and the expansion of Medicaid, the level of the uninsured population in the target communities is significantly higher than that of the state.

[TEXT] Barriers that impact the service area population's access to healthcare, in addition to poverty, include a lower level of education and a higher rate of unemployment than the state. The Census indicates 45.6% of the FPCSN service area population over 25 had a high school diploma or less, compared to 41.3% in the state. U.S. Bureau of Labor Statistics data indicate the average rate of unemployment in the state was 3.6% in 2024, but an estimate can be made for the area of a 5.3% unemployment rate based on extrapolation by the demographic makeup of the population7.

[HEADING] Prenatal and Perinatal Health

[TEXT] The United States has the worst maternal mortality rate among wealthy countries, with a rate three times higher than the next countries14,15. A study of Maternal Mortality found that Philadelphia's rate of pregnancy-related deaths was 15% higher than the national rate. Deaths due to accidental drug overdoses increased from 25% of Philadelphia's pregnancy-associated deaths (2010-16) to 39% (2017-18)16, which a City report traced to lack of access in healthcare and social service resources available to prenatal and postpartum women, complications in coordination of care, and behavioral health problems, including mental illness and drug abuse. The FPCSN service area population experiences disproportionately poor health outcomes as compared to the state in the areas of infant mortality, low birth weight, prenatal care, and breastfeeding initiation, which subsequently impact the overall health, including cognitive and behavioral development of young children. Pennsylvania health statistics extrapolated for the service area indicate the percentage of births to women who did not receive prenatal care in the first trimester of their pregnancies was 38.8%, significantly higher than the state rate of 27.5%, and 5.6% of births were to teen mothers, compared to 3.3%. There were 12.1% of babies in the service area born at a low birth weight and the infant mortality rate was 8.1/1,000 births, compared to state rates of 8.4% and 5.6/1,00017. Of the 12,794 mothers in the service area who gave birth in 2023, 59.4% were living below poverty, compared to 54.2% in the City and 37.7% in the state18.

[HEADING] Behavioral Health

[TEXT] The service area experiences higher levels of mental health problems than the state, with higher levels of reported poor mental health and depression. BRFSS data indicates that the percentage of adults reporting that their mental health was not good more than 14 days in the past month was 16.8% in the service area, compared to 14.9% in the state, and 23.3% indicated that they had at some point been diagnosed with a depressive disorder, compared to 21.7% in the state. Drug overdose/poisoning deaths are a serious problem in Pennsylvania and in Philadelphia, and the rates have been increasing tremendously. Philadelphia's fatal overdose rate was the second worst among the nation's large cities in 2020 and it doubled since 201419. The majority of individuals with a substance use disorder never access specialty substance use treatment20. Pennsylvania Health statistics indicate the rate of drug-induced mortality in Philadelphia in 2023 was still twice the rate in the state.

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] It is estimated that about 26% of people in the country have an anxiety, mood, impulse control, and/or substance abuse disorder in a given year21. Studies indicate that more than half of the adult population has medical conditions and a quarter have mental health disorders and that medical conditions often overlap with mental health disorders22. The presence of multiple conditions complicates treatment, continuing care, and outcomes.

[HEADING] Oral Health

[TEXT] Pennsylvania has a number of issues related to oral health. Using extrapolation from the BRFSS and the Data Resource Center for Child and Adolescent Health, it can be estimated 36.1% of adults and 14.5% of children in the area received no dental care in the past year, compared to 32.3% and 15.6% for the state23,24. Access to affordable oral health services and anxiety are two significant reasons people do not receive dental care. Many people in the areas served reason to be apprehensive, given that they have received mostly emergency care, with long painful waits, treatment drawn out over a number of visits, and service at unfamiliar and unsatisfactory places. Financial barriers to dental care also have a negative impact. People who cannot afford care may wait until only extraction will resolve the problem or may choose extraction if it is the only alternative to the cost of a root canal, build-up, and crown. Many of the patients seen in the service area have limited education and understanding of the importance of oral health care.

[HEADING] Health of Residents of Public Housing

[TEXT] As noted above, FPCSN focuses its services on residents of public housing and the surrounding low-income areas. A study of people relocated as a result of the HOPE VI program25, which was designed to address problems in distressed public housing, indicated that the health of those in the mostly female HOPE VI sample has been significantly worse than the nation. Forty-one percent reported their health was "fair or poor", a rate three times that of US adults and about twice that of women nationally. The rates of the five most prevalent health problems: obesity, hypertension, diabetes, arthritis, and asthma, were significantly higher among the HOPE VI women. A follow up article26 indicated that the overall health of the HOPE VI women remained significantly poorer than that of adults and women nationally. Depression and stroke were added to the list of physical health problems experienced by the HOPE VI women at significantly higher rates than national prevalence rates.

[HEADING] How Area Health Care Needs Are Being Met

[TEXT] a. How Comprehensive Health Care Service Needs Are Being Met Despite Philadelphia's tremendous medical resources, significant health problems continue for the service area population. A Philadelphia Department of Public Health (PDPH) report on

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] Access to Primary Care27 indicated that the total number of primary care providers in the City continues to rise, but the proportion of providers who accept Medicaid has declined in recent years, leaving areas with low income minority populations with lower numbers of PCPs, fewer available primary care appointments, and longer wait times for people with Medicaid, compared to those who are privately insured. Citywide, 85% of providers surveyed had an appointment available for a patient with private insurance and 66% had availability for a patient with Medicaid. The availability percentage for the West and Southwest Districts were 76% and 50%. Wait times for new patients were nine days for private pay and 16 days for those covered by Medicaid, the greatest disparity among the cities studied in the report. The recommendations in the report were that PDPH health systems and FQHCs locate primary care facilities in the noted underserved areas, including the areas that FPCSN serves.

[TEXT] The Philadelphia service area has 14 FQHCs that have sites across the City and adjacent Delaware County, as well as eight FQHCs and Look-Alikes operated by the PDPH. (These numbers include FPCSN as it operated as part of RHD.) Even with all of these resources, only 38.7% of the low-income people in the FPCSN service area accessed FQHC services in 2023, leaving 298,589 low-income people unserved, including 43,404 uninsured people. In the Delaware County Zip Codes, the penetration rate was only 25.2%. All of the FQHCs and the City Health District Centers provide the full range of required primary and preventive services. Many of the FQHCs provide brief behavioral health services integrated with primary care, but only FPCSN and one other provide traditional outpatient BH services. Five of the FQHCs, including FPCSN and the City Centers, provide dental services as well. Only FPCSN and one other FQHC provide obstetric services directly. In the City Centers, patients experience great difficulty receiving care in a timely manner. Extrapolated data from the BRFSS survey28 indicate that 12.1% of service area adults do not have a personal health care provider, and 13.7% of adults needed to see a doctor in the last year but could not because of cost.

[TEXT] In the greater Philadelphia area, fourteen hospitals have closed since 1992, nine in the past seven years, and 15 of Philadelphia's 19 obstetric units have closed since 1997, resulting in a reduction of more than 50% in the number of obstetric beds, and leaving most of these in the more affluent areas29. A hospital that served the nearby Delaware County Zip Codes closed this past spring. The City still has major hospitals remaining in the service area, including the University of Pennsylvania (Penn) Health System with its main hospitals in West Philadelphia, Roxborough Hospital, Temple University Hospital, and the Children's Hospital of Philadelphia (CHOP) and St. Christopher's Hospital for Children. All of these hospitals are non-profit institutions with trauma centers, specialty clinics, and inpatient services, pharmacy, x-ray and other ancillary services. FPCSN has an agreement for patient admissions with the Penn Hospital System and Penn Family Medicine, including contracts for prenatal and obstetric services that include admissions for deliveries. All of the hospitals have sliding scales available both in their inpatient and specialty services and FPCSN works with all of them.

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] Philadelphia has significant behavioral health resources. The service system for behavioral health and drug or alcohol services is organized around a public managed care organization, Community Behavioral Health (CBH), which contracts with the state Medicaid program to manage these services. The Department of Behavioral Health and Intellectual disAbility Services (DBHIDS) provides services to people with Medicaid and people who are uninsured through 11 community mental health centers, five crisis response centers, and 30 specialized BH providers, including FPCSN, that serve over 110,000 people annually. The Office of Addiction Services (OAS) contracts with community providers, including FPCSN, to provide prevention and treatment services that include outpatient treatment, detoxification, residential services, and case management, and serves about 24,000 people per year. There are a limited number of spaces available through these organizations for people without insurance, so the services provided by FPCSN and the other FQHCs that focus on low-income people are very important.

[TEXT] Pennsylvania has 149 Dental Health Professional Shortage Areas, including four that almost cover Philadelphia, and the number of dental health professionals is expected to decrease in the coming years. With these shortages, only 48% of the oral health care needs of the population, and only 23% of dental practitioners participate in the Medicaid program, so many of those not receiving care are low-income people30. The reason there are such broad Dental HPSAs in the City is that many practices do not accept Medicaid and most do not have sliding scales for people with low income. So again, the services provided by FPCSN and the other FQHCs that focus on low-income people are very important.

[TEXT] In-Scope Chronic Disease Management, Cancer Screening, Health Education, Nutrition - Many primary care practices that operate in the City and nearby Delaware County, including those operated by the health systems, the FQHCs, and private providers, provide comprehensive medical care that includes cancer screening and chronic disease management. What often is lacking in practices other than FPCSN and the other FQHCs is a broader view of health care and an awareness of the needs of lower income patients for preventive health education, nutrition information and assistance, and help to overcome barriers to health, such as food insecurity, housing instability, and financial strain. These issues of food/housing insecurity and financial issues need to take precedence before people can work on their health problems. Most health systems and private medical practices do not have these issues at the forefront of their work with patients and they also usually do not have mental health services integrated with primary care.

[TEXT] Public Housing Residents Health Needs

[TEXT] FPCSN has worked closely with the Philadelphia Housing Authority (PHA) in providing care and support for residents in public housing. Established in 1937, PHA is the nation's fourth largest public housing authority. It owns and operates more than 14,000 affordable housing units and serves more than 80,000 Philadelphians. The majority of PHA housing is in what is termed "conventional" public housing, that is, multi-family developments that may have anywhere from under 20 to several hundred units. PHA has moved away from conventional high-rise public housing to incorporate family, senior, and mixed developments that comprise about 7,600 of the units in the system. In addition, PHA is unique among housing authorities nationwide in that it also owns over 6,400 "Scattered Site" housing units that are located throughout Philadelphia.

[TEXT] 30 Pennsylvania Department of Health, PA Oral Health Pla 2020-2030, https://paoralhealth.org/resource_type/ pennsylvania-department-of-health/

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] PHA prides itself on the level of resident involvement in Philadelphia public housing. There are active Resident Councils, not only in the developments, but also for areas of scattered site housing. The Resident Councils are supported by Tenant Support Services, a subsidiary of PHA that is operated by tenants. Through a Resident Advisory Board that includes representatives of all of the Councils, residents participate actively in PHA policy development. Working with the Tenant Support Services, PHA publishes a quarterly newsletter that goes to all households, sponsors an online information portal for residents, and sponsors health fairs and a Food for Life fresh produce program. All of these initiatives can be vehicles that FPCSN is able to use to inform PHA residents of the services available.

[TEXT] As noted above, the low-income populations who are residents of public housing have significant barriers to good health. PHA does not directly provide health services to residents of its public housing. One effect of the scattered site housing in Philadelphia is that those public housing residents are more difficult to reach than those in conventional developments. PHA refers people in need of health care to FPCSN, the Philadelphia Department of Public Health, and the other public housing FQHC. FPCSN is also the health partner for PHA for several of their recent federal projects with the Department of Housing and Urban Development.

[TEXT] FPCSN's three comprehensive sites are located near and work with PHA developments. The Abbottsford Falls site serves Abbottsford Homes, the 11th Street site serves Richard Allen Homes as well as others, and the Health Annex serves Bartram Village. Each of these sites maintains an active Community Advisory Committee that includes members who are current or former public housing residents, which meet monthly and advise on clinic operations, patient experience, and emerging community needs. In collaboration with local Tenant Councils, the Abbottsford Falls and Bartram Village sites have provided mobile health, mind-body wellness offerings, perinatal health resources, and food pantry initiatives that strengthen local engagement and advance health access. At 11th Street, FPCSN partners with the Richard Allen community and the John F. Street Community Center on walking groups and cooking-based nutrition education with Vetri Community Partnership. To strengthen these collaborations, FPCSN has recruited a PHA representative to serve on its Board of Directors, ensuring resident perspectives help guide the Network's strategic trajectory and governance priorities.

[TEXT] b/c. How Patient Support Services and Other Needs that Impact Health Status Are Being Met The population served by FPCSN is poor and has many health-related social needs. There are many people in the area who are food insecure; 15.8% of people and 30.9% of children in Philadelphia, compared to 8.9% and 13.1% for the state31. A Pew report found that 40% of all households and 88% of low-income households are housing cost-burdened - spending more than 30% of their income on housing32. A report by Community Legal Services found that the level of housing insecurity increased during the pandemic33. The situation is similar in Delaware County, where a family of four needs $97,000 to meet basic costs, the median income in the area served by FPCSN was $56,454, and 18.4% of households are housing cost-burdened, compared to

[TEXT] 31 Philly Voice, Food Insecurity Increased in the Philly Region During the Covid-19 Pandemic,

[TEXT] 15.8% for the County. The Foundation for Delaware County estimates that 12% of children and over 43,000 people overall are food insecure34. Again, other than FPCSN and the other FQHCs, there are few health services in the service area that take these things into consideration in the care they provide.

[TEXT] People in the areas served not only require assistance with the barriers to health posed by food insecurity, housing insecurity, and financial strain. They also need health education, particularly to help them prevent and manage chronic illnesses, education about nutrition and how to prepare healthy food on a budget, and help to manage multiple health care providers and regimes. Care coordination services, such as those provided by FPCSN, are essential to supporting patients in understanding and finding ways to follow through with the care they need. Case management, care coordination, and assistance in the community are often necessary to help some people to maintain and manage their health. Other than FPCSN and the other FQHCs, almost no health services in the area have these kinds of services.

[TEXT] The FPCSN population in Philadelphia has a number of problems traveling to medical services, even in a large city with a transportation system. The public transit system has recently made cuts to services that exacerbate these problems. The public transportation needed to reach health services can be costly and difficult for parents with several children or for elderly people. There are neighborhood-defining streets, railroad corridors, and rivers that act as de facto barriers, as well as dangerous areas through which women in particular do not wish to travel, especially with children. Many of the families served have a number of children, cannot afford child care, and must bring children to appointments, and many have significant health problems that limit their mobility. These same issues exist for the patients coming from Delaware County, which is served by the same public transit authority. It is essential that health providers assist patients with the cost of transportation or with other services to help them when needed. Few providers in the service area other than FPCSN and the other FQHCs do so.

[HEADING] 3. Recent or Potential Changes in the Health Care Landscape

[TEXT] The health care landscape in Southeastern Pennsylvania is changing quickly and dramatically. As more private equity has become part of health care, many hospitals have closed or become for-profit in recent years, including almost three quarters of the obstetric units in the area, leaving most of those remaining in the more affluent areas. Some patients have to travel significant distances to get to emergency care or to prenatal and obstetric services, which is particularly difficult for low-income patients. The Medicaid unwinding and further cuts that are possible will leave many of the people in the low-income population that FPCSN serves without access to health care coverage or with coverage that leaves them with high deductibles and co-pays. This will require FPCSN and all of the community health centers to provide assistance and the outreach and enrollment staff will be critical to health centers' ability to serve the community.

[TEXT] FPCSN and the FQHCs are also working to adjust to the change to Value-based payment methodology and the focus on HEDIS measures. The population served experiences significant health problems and barriers to care and positive outcomes, making it challenging to adjust to outcomes-based payment mechanisms. The additional care coordination needed to manage in this

[TEXT] 34 The Foundation for Delaware County, https://delcofoundation.org/our-impact/priority-areas/food-security/

[TEXT] 10



========== PAGE 21 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] system will benefit the patients that FPCSN serves. However, an increase in staffing is necessary to be successful at value-based care and that is a barrier to implementation of this methodology. FPCSN has joined its first Accountable Care Organization, a small ACO focused on Medicare patients, as it works to build the necessary infrastructure to work in the new environment.

[TEXT] Another payment change the center is working to adjust to is the restrictions the pharmaceutical companies are putting on discounts on drugs distributed through contracted pharmacies, as well as the increasing reporting that is being required. FPCSN has made adjustments to manage the restrictions, but the administrative tasks again require added staffing. The revenue generated in the 340b program goes back into health center to support services, particularly enabling services, so reductions in this revenue have an impact on the services available to patients. On the positive side, the addition of enhanced payments to FQHCs for long-acting reversible contraception will increase the health center's capacity to provide these to patients.

[TEXT] FPCSN continues to adapt to a dynamic health care environment shaped by pending changes to ensure access to primary care, behavioral health, and enabling services across its service area. Guided by its Strategic Plan and Continuous Quality Improvement Plan, FPCSN is preparing to align its operations with these changes by strengthening community partnerships and integrating social support services into care delivery.

[HEADING] 4. How Projected Numbers of Patients were Determined

[TEXT] a/b. Unduplicated Patients and Patients by Type of Service Expected to be Served in 2026 The health center served 14,872 unduplicated patients in 2022, 15,214 patients in 2023, and 15,617 patients in 2024. FPCSN's continuing services and projects will serve at least 15,617 patients by the close of 2026, the same as the Patient Target advertised in the SAAT, and has used that number in the process of developing the budget and Form 1A. This unduplicated number of patients to be served includes 12,839 patients provided with 41,085 medical visits, 3,966 patients provided with 31,339 mental health visits, 78 patients provided with 174 substance abuse services, 3,569 patients provided with 8,208 dental visits and 4,300 patients provided with 7,375 enabling visits. Although FPCSN expects to be able to serve the target number of patients overall, there may be fewer patients served in dental and behavioral health services as staff need to be hired in those departments. The health center will be continuing to work to serve more patients as it is clear that the need in the Philadelphia area is tremendous.

[HEADING] 5. Community Needs Assessment and Input

[HEADING] a. How Area Needs will be Assessed and Updated

[TEXT] FPCSN policy requires that an assessment of the needs of the target population and the service area communities will be conducted at least every three years and will be used by management and the Board for strategic planning. The needs assessment is to include information from the Census, state and local studies, statistical analysis, and information obtained through the relationships the health center and its Board members have in the community served. The needs assessment process also uses information from FPCSN's performance improvement activities, which includes utilization and outcomes data, risk management and incident information, and patient satisfaction surveys and surveys of and visioning sessions with staff about whether patient needs are being met and any suggestions about how services can be improved.

[TEXT] 11



========== PAGE 22 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[HEADING] b. How the Scope of Project will be Updated as Needed

[TEXT] Any information that comes from the needs assessment process or from patient or community satisfaction surveys regarding a need for services that are lacking in the service area will be investigated and, in some cases, responded to with new services being initiated and added to the Scope of Project. Likewise, the assessment of patient origins conducted each year, as noted above, may find that a significant number of patients are coming from an area not in scope and this may result in an analysis of that area and why the change and influx might be taking place. In some cases, this may result in a Scope of Project update to add new areas.

[TEXT] c. How Patient and Community Input will Inform and Improve Service Delivery

[TEXT] The goal of FPCSN's needs assessment and quality improvement activities is to continuously improve services and assure that they are meeting the needs of the service area and the target population. Information about patients' use of health center services and what else they need is gathered through patient satisfaction surveys and sometimes through focus groups as well. Staff opinions are also surveys as they have close contact with patients and know their needs. The patient members on Board share their experiences and opinions as well. In addition, each of the comprehensive health center sites has a Community Advisory Committee made up of people from the area surrounding the site, some of whom live in public housing, the mission of which is to help the site understand the needs and opinions of the community. Finally, FPCSN collaborates with community organizations, such as the FQHCs and the Pennsylvania Association of Community Health Centers, the hospitals, and the Public Housing Authority and the School District, to assure that pertinent information is gathered regarding the health care landscape, needs of the community, and opinion regarding the work of the health center.

[TEXT] FPCSN embeds patient and community feedback into all aspects of service design and performance improvement through its Strategic Plan and Continuous Quality Improvement framework. Each health center operates a Community Advisory Committee composed of patients and local residents who meet regularly with site leadership to share experiences, identify community needs, and inform program priorities. Patient input is also collected through satisfaction surveys and patient town halls that provide opportunities for open dialogue. Data from these sources are analyzed quarterly by the leadership to identify trends and inform targeted quality improvement initiatives that address access, communication, and service delivery. At the governance level, FPCSN's Board of Directors, which includes 12 patient and community representatives, works with management to review patient feedback to guide strategic direction and ensure accountability to the populations served. Together, these structures create a continuous feedback loop where community voices shape priorities, inform CQI projects, and strengthen equitable, patient-centered care.

[HEADING] RESPONSE

[HEADING] 1. How Service Delivery Sites Will Support Access to Care

[TEXT] FPCSN began 33 years ago in three converted apartments in a public housing development, and now consists of three fixed, permanent sites that provide all required and in-scope additional services: Abbottsford/Falls, the Health Annex, and the Stephen and Sandra Sheller 11th Street Family Health Services of Drexel University, as well as six smaller, seasonal school-based sites.

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[HEADING] a. Location of Service Delivery Sites

[TEXT] Abbottsford/Falls Health Center 4700 Wissahickon Avenue Philadelphia, Pennsylvania 19144

[TEXT] The Abbottsford/Falls Health Center was the first FPCSN site and is co-located with the administrative offices of the FPCSN. The site is now four blocks from the Abbottsford Homes Housing Development and 1.8 miles from the Falls Development. The site provides comprehensive services and has 12 examining rooms, 7 behavioral health rooms, 7 dental operatories, and additional staff space and break out space for behavioral health services, health and diabetic education, and group services. Patients who need assistance coming to the site, particularly those at Abbottsford Homes who have mobility difficulties, are helped to access Medicaid transportation or public transportation. The Rite Choice Pharmacy that contracts to manage the Network's Title X 340B pharmaceuticals and fills prescriptions for patients is located in a shop adjacent to the health center. Contract specialists provide podiatry, cardiology, physical therapy, and ophthalmology services on-site.

[TEXT] 11th Street Health Center 859 North 11th Street Philadelphia, Pennsylvania 19123

[TEXT] The Stephen and Sandra Sheller 11th Street Family Health Services of Drexel University became part of the Network through a New Access Point grant at the time the facility opened in 2002. It had been in operation since 1998 in a smaller facility nearby. The 11th Street site is operated by FPCSN under an operational agreement with Drexel University College of Nursing and Health Professions. The 11th Street Health Center is located in an area of North Philadelphia that is known as the 11th Street Public Housing Corridor because of the concentration of public housing located there. There are almost 3,000 people living in four surrounding public housing developments, and many patients walk to the health center, which also serves as a community center. There is public transportation near the site. The 11th Street site has 16 exam rooms and 9 dental operatories and provides comprehensive services. Rite Choice Pharmacy has a location within the health center. Contract specialists provide podiatry, cardiology, and ophthalmology services on-site. Cooking classes and other services are also operated under private grants.

[TEXT] Health Annex Health Center 6120 Woodland Avenue Philadelphia, Pennsylvania 19142

[TEXT] In 2003, FPCSN received a New Access Point grant and took on the operation of the Health Annex health center from the University of Pennsylvania School of Nursing, which had operated it since 1995. The Health Annex is located in the Woodland Shopping Center, which is located between the two public housing developments in a densely populated low-income area, on a trolley line, and accessible by several means of public transportation. The site provides comprehensive services and has 16 examining rooms, 6 behavioral health rooms, and 6 dental operatories, as well as a fitness center and physical therapy. Contract specialists on-site provide podiatry, cardiology, physical therapy, and optometry services. A Rite Choice Pharmacy is located within the health center.

They are permanent sites and are noted as seasonal because they are closed for a few weeks in the summer. They are open during school hours whenever the school is in session. The sites provide primary care, behavioral health, and enabling/health education services in collaboration with school nurses and counseling staff. Patients needing outpatient mental health, dental care, or specialty services are assisted to make appointments at one of the full FPCSN sites. Patients at the school-based sites have access to FPCSN 24 hours and they have access to the other FPCSN sites and services when school is not in session.

[HEADING] b. How Barriers Are Addressed

[TEXT] As noted above regarding each site, the FPCSN health centers are in locations that are accessible to public housing developments managed by the Philadelphia Housing Authority, either by walking or by public transportation. The school-based sites make care available to students in the place where they spend much of their time. Those sites, which do not provide dental and other specialty services, are within easy travel of the comprehensive sites. Patients with mobility difficulties or who must bring children to appointments are assisted to access transportation operated by their insurer, or provided transit passes to travel on public transportation. The hours noted are appropriate for the patient population. All three comprehensive sites have walk-in availability. Patients are able to make same-day appointments for urgent problems and appointments within 48 hours for non-urgent needs. All of the comprehensive FPCSN health centers are open 40 hours or more and have evening and Saturday hours to accommodate the needs and schedules of as many patients as possible. Hours are determined in consultation with the FPCSN Community Advisory Committees, taking into consideration the responses to patient satisfaction surveys, and are approved by the Board.

[HEADING] c. Competing Supplement Applicants - NA

[HEADING] 2. Provision of Required and Additional Health Services

[TEXT] FPCSN has used an integrated model to deliver primary care provided by nurse practitioner, behavioral health, dental, and preventive services providers, along with other team members. The FPCSN model is a psychosocial, interdisciplinary approach that "wraps its arms" around patients. FPCSN's health centers provide all of the required primary care services, as well as integrated and outpatient behavioral health, dental, and wellness services. All of the services on Form 5A are provided directly, except for radiology and intrapartum care, and are described below in roughly the order they are found on the form.

[HEADING] a. Access to Required and Additional Services by All Age Groups

[HEADING] General Primary Medical Care, Screenings and Immunizations

[TEXT] Pediatric and Well-Child Services: Well-baby and well-child visits, including height and weight and developmental assessments; immunizations; lead levels and other diagnostic testing; vision and hearing screens; dental screening; screening for family distress; and parent education, are provided directly in all of the FPCSN comprehensive health centers. All FPCSN providers have training regarding screening for family violence, whether against children or among partners in the home. Children and their families receive counseling either integrated with their primary health visits or through outpatient services on-site. Children receive almost all dental services in their center or referral to the nearest FPCSN comprehensive site. Children are referred to collaborating providers for diagnostic imaging and other higher-level testing, and for early intervention developmental and other specialty clinical services.

[TEXT] Adolescent Services: Physical exams, including height and weight, vision and hearing screens, education regarding high-risk behavior issues (tobacco usage, sexually transmitted infections and pregnancy, substance abuse, use of seat belts), depression screening, and age-appropriate immunizations and labs for adolescents are provided in all of the health center sites, including the school-based sites. Adolescents and their families receive counseling either integrated with their primary health visits or through outpatient services at the comprehensive sites. They are referred to other providers for substance abuse treatment beyond what can be managed in outpatient counseling or through the FPCSN MOUD program. Adolescents who are patients in the school sites are referred for full dental care at the nearest comprehensive site. Young people are referred to collaborating providers for diagnostic imaging and other higher-level testing, as well as specialty clinical services.

[TEXT] Adult Services: All FPCSN comprehensive health center sites provide adult routine sick and health maintenance exams, including vital signs, height and weight; hearing and vision screens; immunizations as indicated; age-appropriate labs; prostate and breast exams and other cancer screens; and counseling/education regarding health issues such as weight management, nutrition, tobacco use, and substance abuse. Podiatry, cardiology, and optometry services are provided on site in the comprehensive centers through contracts and referral agreements. Behavioral health counseling, including psychiatric medication prescription, is provided either as part of primary care visits or through outpatient services. The large sites have psychiatric nurse practitioners embedded in primary care. Patients receive almost all dental services in their center. Mind/body services, such as massage, pain management, and yoga, are provided by FPCSN mind/body educators. Adults are referred to collaborating providers for diagnostic imaging, ultrasound, and other higher-level testing, and for specialty care, including inpatient and intensive mental health and substance abuse treatment. An area mobile mammogram van does periodic screenings at the health centers. An FPCSN HIV care specialist treats patients and consults with other FPCSN providers when they are caring for HIV-positive patients.

[TEXT] Geriatric Services: Services for elderly people include routine adult services as noted above; immunizations as indicated; age-appropriate labs; functional assessments; counseling/education that includes home safety and osteoporosis prevention; and case management to assure patients have the services they need to remain safely and in good health at home.

[HEADING] Diagnostic Laboratory and Diagnostic Radiology

[TEXT] All of the FPCSN sites have laboratories that perform all in-house tests allowable by CLIA, in accordance with standards, including the school sites. Blood samples are drawn by health center staff and, for tests involving blood analysis that cannot be performed in-house, the samples are sent to designated managed care laboratories. In-house tests with immediate results include HbA1c levels and pregnancy tests, hemoglobin, strep, flu, and covid tests. Staff are trained in rapid HIV testing and brief counseling. Patients requiring x-ray services and other diagnostic procedures are referred to collaborating local hospitals, including CHOP and St. Christopher's for children and the Penn hospitals for adults.

[TEXT] Family Planning, Gynecological, Prenatal, Obstetrical, and Postpartum Services

[TEXT] Voluntary family planning, pregnancy testing, and routine gynecological care are provided on- site at all FPCSN sites, including those in the schools. FPCSN has a contract with the University of Pennsylvania Department of Family Medicine & Community Health (Penn Medicine) for the on-site provision of prenatal and obstetrical care and deliveries are managed in the Penn hospitals. Patients are referred to Penn Medicine for specialty gynecological services and prenatal services for women with high-risk pregnancies. Postpartum care is provided in the comprehensive health center sites.

[TEXT] Preventive and Additional Dental Services

[TEXT] The first Network Dental Center opened in December 2004 in the 11th Street site. The Health Annex Dental Center opened in 2008 and the Abbottsford/Falls Dental Center opened in 2010. The FPCSN dental centers are built around the concept of providing patients of all ages with a "Dental Home", a familiar and non-threatening environment where they can receive most of their care. The services provided in the FPCSN dental centers include cleaning and root planning and scaling for patients of all ages, and topical fluoridation and sealants for children. Restorative services such as composite and amalgam fillings are provided, as well as emergency services to relieve pain such as extractions, nerve removal, and abscess care. Rehabilitative services, such as

[TEXT] 16



========== PAGE 27 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] crowns, dentures, and endodontics, are provided on-site. FPCSN has at least one full-time certified dental hygienist position at each site to provide comprehensive periodontal hygiene care, preventive services, and follow-up and outreach services when needed. Dental services are fully integrated with the primary medical care provided by the health centers. Screening is done for oral cancers, so that they can be detected as early as possible and treatment can be integrated with the primary care providers and specialists. FPCSN has grant funding available through which uninsured patients receive root canal and other procedures.

[TEXT] Efforts are made to bring primary care patients into dental services. Through the "Happy Visits" program, children who come in for well-child visits, women who come in for prenatal visits are encouraged to go to the dental department on the same day as their visit and are seen on that day whenever possible. Mothers of infants are educated in ways to care for their babies' teeth and the importance of doing so. FPCSN has partnered with the MCOs to hold well-child days that include visits in both primary care and dental services.

[TEXT] Full dental services as described here are planned in the budget for this SAC. Currently, only one FPCSN dental clinic is operating because the upheaval and uncertainty of the separation from RHD and loss of FQHC status caused many of the dental staff to leave and seek positions elsewhere.

[HEADING] Pharmaceutical Services

[TEXT] The FPCSN health centers work to assure that all patients who do not have prescription coverage can afford necessary medications. FPCSN has long had an arrangement with the Rite Choice Pharmacy to manage health center medications through the 340B Federal Drug Pricing Program and provide these to patients. Rite Choice is continuing to provide patients with discounted medications partly supported by FPCSN's Title X 340B Program. Rite Choice provides integrated pharmacy services inside or adjacent to each of the comprehensive sites. The pharmacy manages medications purchased by health, packages and labels them for patients needing to have prescriptions filled, and charges patients $6.00, which is waived if the patient is unable to pay. FPCSN is a Title X grantee and has a contract with Access Matters of Southeastern Pennsylvania that provides funding for pharmaceuticals related to contraception, cervical cancer screening and screening and treatment of sexually transmitted diseases.

[HEADING] Health Education and Nutrition Services

[TEXT] Health and nutrition education are a major focus of the FPCSN health centers. The population served by FPCSN has high rates of diabetes and obesity and many are now being prescribed GLP-1 medications and need a significant amount education and support in their use. The nurse practitioners and the RNs in the primary care teams incorporate nutrition education into primary care visits. In addition, FPCSN plans to have nutritionists, including some who are certified diabetic educators, to work in all of the health centers. Nutrition services are also provided by Community Health Workers in patients' homes, when directed by the nurse practitioner responsible for primary care. In addition, FPCSN has food pantries and WIC is on-site at each of the three comprehensive sites to help address food insecurity.

[HEADING] Outreach, Eligibility Assistance, and Case Management Services

[TEXT] FPCSN has a community engagement department that works to develop and maintain relationships with diverse community organizations to assure the broadest possible awareness of the health center's services and collaboration to provide the most seamless service to the target communities. Outreach and Enrollment staff work in the community to assist people to

[TEXT] 17



========== PAGE 28 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] find and connect with all health insurance coverage they are eligible for. These staff work with front desk staff and determine patients' eligibility for medical coverage when they come to one of the health centers without insurance. The health center also contracts with Fabric Health, a service that helps families apply for or renew public benefits and use their health benefits.

[TEXT] FPCSN screens all patients receiving care for health-related social needs to determine their need for social and support services. Social Workers provide patients with referrals to a variety of community services, such as income support, employment assistance, in-home services, and Meals-on-Wheels and other types of food assistance. Social workers and front desk staff work with the primary care teams to follow up when patients miss appointments, through telephone calls, and outreach efforts, as appropriate, with family members and significant others. These efforts are focused on determining the reasons for missed appointments and taking the steps needed to ensure that subsequent appointments are kept.

[TEXT] There are Care Coordinators at each site who work with patients who have chronic conditions to assure they receive and required specialist care and that they are seen regularly to monitor their conditions. The Care Coordinators work with the primary care teams and with Community Health Workers to be sure that patients understand and are able to follow their medical regimens. They also follow up on patients who have had emergency room visits or hospitalizations to assure there is a plan in place to assure that they have the medications and supports they need and they are seen in the health center soon after their discharge.

[TEXT] Transportation and Translation Services

[TEXT] As noted above, the FPCSN population has a number of problems traveling to medical services, even in a large city with a transportation system. The public transit system has recently announced changes that will exacerbate these problems. Social service staff may provide patients with transit passes or help them access all Medicaid transportation assistance that is available to them. FPCSN also has an account with UberHealth to provide transportation for patients in urgent situations.

[TEXT] FPCSN makes every effort to be sensitive and responsive to the linguistic issues presented by the people it serves. The FPCSN is compliant with federal requirements regarding Limited English Proficiency. FPCSN policy requires that no person will be denied access to services based solely on his/her inability to communicate aurally or orally in English. Each FPCSN site has Spanish speakers and there are staff who speak a number of different languages. Some referring organizations send translators with patients. The health center contracts with a language line for translators and interpreters for people with hearing deficiencies.

[HEADING] Additional Mental Health and Substance Use Disorder Services

[TEXT] FPCSN has had an outpatient behavioral health department as part of its practice since its inception and FPCSN was among the first area FQHCs to integrate behavioral health services into primary care, beginning in 2008. Both integrated and outpatient BH services are provided on-site in the comprehensive sites and integrated services are provided in the school sites. Behavioral health services are provided by licensed clinical social workers and clinical psychologists, as well as psychiatrists. FPCSN psychiatric nurse practitioners provide medication management for depression, bipolar disorder, anxiety, ADD/ADHD, schizophrenia, and schizoaffective disorder, working with both primary care and integrated BH providers.

[TEXT] 18



========== PAGE 29 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[TEXT] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] Integrated services are provided by Behavioral Health Consultants (BHCs) who work side-by- side with nurse practitioners, in the exam room, in all FPCSN sites. The goal of these Integrated Behavioral Health services is to develop and implement, within primary care, brief problem- focused treatment plans that include both medical and behavioral interventions focused on restoration of function and quality of life. Patients are taught behavioral strategies to help them adhere to medical regimens, new coping mechanisms, stress reduction strategies, and pain and/or disease management techniques. Patients receive behavioral health follow-up visits within the primary care area of the health center. Obtaining behavioral health in the same exam room where they receive physical health care normalizes the experience for patients, provides on-the-spot care in a familiar space, and dispels the stigma often associated with mental health care. Working with the BHCs, many patients are able to stabilize and manage both their behavioral health problems and their physical health. Integrated BHCs also provide screening for substance abuse using a specialized tool to assess whether screening, treatment, and referral for substance abuse in the primary care setting is the treatment option best suited for the patient.

[TEXT] FPCSN has a Medication for Opioid Use Disorder (MOUD) program and five providers are able to prescribe Vivitrol and Suboxone. When patients present for MOUD treatment, they are connected with the FPCSN program, or another organization through the Office of Behavioral Health if there is reason to do so, such as the need for detox. When the patient receives MOUD treatment with an FPCSN primary care provider, at each visit they see a BHC who coordinates the multiple services that people using mental health or substance use services often need, including the mind/body, pain management, nutrition, social work, and other integrated services available within FPCSN.

[TEXT] Patients with serious behavioral health conditions and with substance use disorders that require treatment that is more intensive than can be addressed by the health centers are referred to local organizations that provide intensive and/or in-patient services, including:

[TEXT]  Charter Fairmount Behavioral Health System and Friends Hospital for psychiatric inpatient and partial hospitalization services;

[TEXT]  Fairmount Behavioral Health System for acute and subacute inpatient psychiatric, detoxification, rehabilitation, short- and long-term residential, and outpatient chemical dependency and methadone maintenance services;

[TEXT]  Gaudenzia for short- and long-term residential, and outpatient chemical dependency services; and

[TEXT]  Horizon House for Intellectual and Developmental Disability, Behavioral Health, and Homeless Services.

[HEADING] Services by Referral

[TEXT] FPCSN has contract arrangements with psychiatrist, podiatrist, and cardiologist providers, as well as a referral agreement with a physical therapy provider, under which these specialists are provided with space and referrals in the health centers, with the understanding that they will serve patients who are uninsured using the FPCSN sliding scales. In addition, pulmonology, oncology, endocrinology, orthopedics, urology, pain management, neuropsychology; ancillary services, such as audiology; emergency services; and other specialty services are provided through referral to nearby collaborating hospitals and other providers.

[TEXT] b. Access to Services Not Provided In-Person at all Sites

[TEXT] The three FPCSN comprehensive health center sites directly provide all required services, except diagnostic radiology and intrapartum services, and all in-scope additional services. There are some services that are shared among sites. For instance, the lead HIV specialist is located at the Abbottsford Falls but sees HIV patients from all FPCSN sites via telehealth. Some patients may be referred to see her there or she may consult with the patient and/or the primary care provider at another site virtually if her expertise is needed. Many outpatient behavioral health visits are virtual, including those for substance use disorder care, and many patients prefer virtual services. Patients in the school-based sites receive primary care, family planning, and integrated behavioral health care on site. Those needing dental care or outpatient behavioral health services are assisted to make appointments at one of the comprehensive FPCSN sites. Patients at the school-based sites, like all patients, have access to FPCSN 24 hours and they have access to the other FPCSN sites and services when school is not in session.

[HEADING] c. Provision of Services To Maintain and Improve Patient Health

[TEXT] FPCSN provides the patient-centered chronic disease prevention and management, preventive health services and education, cancer screening, nutrition, mental health, and patient support services that many patients require to overcome barriers to their health. The health center focuses on preventive services and health education, assisting patients to understand what is needed to maintain health and avoid chronic illness. The primary care providers and teams of additional staff provide integrated care that includes health education to patients who need it. They are encouraged to have cancer screenings and live a healthy lifestyle to maintain their health. Patients may receive services from Social Workers, Care Coordinators, and/or Community Health Workers to help them connect with exercise and other services in the community, understand their medical regimens, and shop for and prepare healthy meals. Patients are encouraged to manage chronic illnesses to prevent worsening of their conditions. The health center uses connective devices such as blood sugar monitors, pulse oximeters for patients who are asthmatic, Bluetooth-enabled blood pressure cuffs, and dopplers for pregnant patients to support patients in these efforts. The health center's integrated and outpatient mental health, Medication for Opioid Use Disorder, and extensive enabling services, including health education, case management, transportation assistance, and translation, are described in detail above. Last year, a quarter of the health center's patients received integrated and/or outpatient behavioral health services and almost a third received at least one enabling service.

[TEXT] In addition to the individual and family behavioral health services described above, FPCSN often provides group support services targeted to specific areas of need. All of these groups are available to all members of the community, whether or not they are health center users. These group services take place periodically and have included a variety of health-related groups, such as grandparents raising young children facilitated by a social worker from a partnering organization, SOWN (supportive older women's network) groups, Drug and Alcohol Recovery Groups, breast cancer survivor groups, Dialectical Behavior Therapy groups, and creative arts groups facilitated by an LCSW and mind-body educator to provide support for women who have experienced violence.

[TEXT] Additional patient support services are described in e. below.

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[HEADING] d. Support of Care Continuity and Coordination, and Clinical Effectiveness

[TEXT] The FPCSN approach to patient care is built on a foundation of continuity, clinical effectiveness, and patient-centered care coordination. To ensure continuity, each patient has a dedicated care team, consisting of a primary care provider, a nurse, and a medical assistant, who serve as a consistent point of contact for all their health needs. Clinical effectiveness by using evidence- based clinical guidelines for chronic disease management and a unified electronic health record that gives all care team members access to patients' complete medical histories. Peer reviews are conducted to monitor how patients are being treated. Patient-centered care coordination is the key to patient care. A personalized care plan is developed in collaboration with each patient, factoring in their health goals, health-related social needs, and preferences. The care coordinators follow up with patients after appointments, help them schedule specialist visits, and connect them with community resources. The care coordinators follow-up with patients who are due for cancer screenings and assist them in navigating getting these done. They also follow up with patients who have had emergency room visits or hospitalizations to ensure there is a follow up plan in place for them to see their FPCSN provider or a specialist, that they understand and are taking their medication, and that they have a support system in place from family or community nursing services. This integrated model ensures our patients receive not only timely and effective treatment but also feel supported and empowered throughout their care.

[TEXT] e. Provision of Patient Support Service to Address Health Related Social Factors

[TEXT] FPCSN recognizes that achieving optimal health outcomes requires a holistic approach that extends beyond clinical care. The health center is committed to addressing the diverse needs that impact a patient's health status, ensuring that services enabling access to care, comprehensive health and nutrition education, and case management are integrated into service delivery. All FPCSN patients are screened for health-related social needs that may be barriers to their use of services or barriers to improvement in their health. Enabling staff use the information gathered to make referrals as needed to a variety of community services, such as in-home services and Meals- on-Wheels, to provide support for patients in the community. In addition to these enabling services, which are also described above, the FPCSN sites have food pantries and WIC is on-site at each of the three comprehensive sites to help address food insecurity.

[TEXT] FPCSN has had legal services available to patients for a number of years under an agreement with the Legal Clinic for the Disabled, which places a lawyer part-time in each of the three comprehensive sites. This Medical-Legal Program is particularly helpful when patients have issues with their landlord and their housing. The lawyer assists patients with other legal issues that are barriers either to their ability to receive or use health care or to overcome civil legal issues that are causing stress and hindering their ability to be well. The lawyer provides brief advice, options counseling, representation or further referral for a range of civil legal issues, including protection from abuse, Supplemental Security Income, Social Security Disability Insurance, Medical Assistance, and child custody/support.

[HEADING] f. Provision of Patient-Centered Care

[TEXT] The FPCSN approach to care is based on the provision of patient-centered care, ensuring every individual receives services tailored to their unique needs and goals. The health center is committed to empowering patients through comprehensive health education, providing them with the knowledge and resources to make informed decisions and actively participate in their health services. A key component of this strategy involves regular huddles, where the integrated

[TEXT] 21



========== PAGE 32 (from PDF footer) ==========

[TEXT] EHB Application Number: 242645

[TEXT] Grant Number: N/A

[HEADING] Family Practice & Counseling Services Network 2026 Service Area Competition Application

[TEXT] care team reviews the status of all patients, including whether they are actively engaged in care or have been lost to follow-up care. These huddles allow the team to proactively identify and address barriers to care, develop targeted solutions to re-engage patients, and ensure no one falls through the cracks. The team works with patients in a collaborative process that facilitates the implementation of a comprehensive care plan that is not only clinically sound but also aligns with the patient's personal preferences and circumstances, ultimately supporting them in achieving their long-term health goals.

[HEADING] g. Additional Services that Address Other Unique Needs

[TEXT] FPCSN uses a psychosocial, interdisciplinary approach to care that "wraps its arms" around patients. Mind/body services, such as massage, pain management, and yoga, are provided by FPCN mind-body educators. Introduced in 2016, these complementary services were among the first provided in community health centers. Providers and all other staff receive training that helps them be sensitive to the difficulties many patients have experienced that affect their ability to connect to services and they refer patients to mental health or mind/body services to assist them.

[HEADING] 3. Expansion of Access to Additional Services

[TEXT] The focus of FPCSN services is on wellness and, given the prevalence of chronic illness in the communities served, management of these conditions. The nurse practitioners and the RNs in the primary care teams incorporate health and nutrition education into primary care visits. In addition, FPCSN plans to have nutritionists, including some who are certified diabetic educators, to work in all of the health centers. Nutrition services are also provided by Community Health Workers in patients' homes, when directed by the nurse practitioner responsible for primary care. In addition, FPCSN has food pantries and WIC is on-site at each of the three comprehensive sites to help address food insecurity. The health center partners with the Drexel College of Nursing and Health Professions for nursing students to provide health and chronic disease education. Because many patients come to care with low incomes and food insecurity, health education is provided that includes nutrition and how to prepare healthy food on a budget, and how to manage multiple health care providers and regimes. Integrated services include behavioral health, either brief interventions or longer-term therapy; case management to assist with health- related issues that may be barriers to care, legal services, group services that provide education and peer support, and complementary services to reduce stress. FPCSN continues to expand access to comprehensive care through strategic partnerships that extend services beyond traditional health delivery. Guided by its Strategic Plan and CQI framework, the Network prioritizes collaboration and innovation to meet the needs of communities across Philadelphia.

[TEXT] The FPCSN Community Engagement Manager leads partnership development with community-based organizations, public housing authorities, schools, and faith-based groups to co-design enabling services such as behavioral health integration, perinatal support, nutrition programming, health education, and mobile outreach. These collaborations enhance referral pathways, strengthen care coordination, and increase access for residents facing social and geographic barriers to care. The FPCSN Board of Directors, which includes community representatives, advances these efforts by leveraging civic, philanthropic, and institutional relationships to align resources and sustain growth. Through key partnerships with the Philadelphia Housing Authority, School District of Philadelphia, Fabric Health and Health Federation of Philadelphia, FPCSN continues to expand behavioral health, preventive care, and social support capacity.
